<span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Interpreting Abnormal Charge–Discharge Plateau Migration in Cu<sub>x</sub>S during Long-Term Cycling</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3961</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3970</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsami.8b18864</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D11%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D3961-3970%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BMater.%2BInterfaces%26author%3DZ.%2BYang%26author%3DT.%2BChen%26author%3DC.%2BWu%26author%3DJ.%2BQu%26author%3DZ.%2BWu%26author%3DX.%2BGuo%26author%3DB.%2BZhong%26author%3DH.%2BLiu%26author%3DS.%2BDou%26title%3DInterpreting%2BAbnormal%2BCharge%25E2%2580%2593Discharge%2BPlateau%2BMigration%2Bin%2BCuxS%2Bduring%2BLong-Term%2BCycling%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsami.8b18864&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c04946&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsami.8b18864&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interpreting Abnormal Charge-Discharge Plateau Migration in CuxS during Long-Term Cycling</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Zuguang; Chen, Ting; Wu, Chunjin; Qu, Jie; Wu, Zhenguo; Guo, Xiaodong; Zhong, Benhe; Liu, Huakun; Dou, Shixue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3961-3970</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Voltage polarization during cycling, the charge potential increase of anode or discharge plateau decrease of cathode, is widely obsd. and would lower the output voltage.  Conversely, an anomalous potential plateau neg. migration phenomenon was obsd. in CuxS anode of Na-ion battery.  The background mechanism was clarified from the switch of intercalation-conversion reactions and structure evolution.  The dynamic cooperation between intercalation and conversion reactions may root the potential plateau neg. migration during cycling.  In the initial stage, the intercalation-type reaction with Na3Cu4S4 and Na4Cu2S3 products at 2.13 and 1.92 V would dominate the early migration process of potential plateaus.  In the 2nd stage, the conversion-type reaction dominated by Na2S and metallic Cu formed at 1.85 and 1.53 V in the later period.  The aforementioned results would provide new perspective on the electrochem. behavior of transition-metal sulfide anode and provide a clue for reducing voltage polarization.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrEG9-ntnLFlLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liNEXsd6LVeTg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXkslKitA%253D%253D&amp;md5=4e44968872488cbf7f4cf0d142249ddf</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da876819cfb325-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45'],'ref46':['cit46'],'ref47':['cit47'],'ref48':['cit48']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries.  The complexity and importance of recycling battery materials is also discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGph4-d2B3Q8DLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhylv3CzQ0jhg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFKlsrjF&amp;md5=977f0fc171bb41af30457715fc0ded72</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.-K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-Ion Batteries: Present and Future</span>. <i>Chem.                         rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhylv3CzQ0jhg"> &gt;&gt;                         rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Transition metal cations on the move: simultaneous operando X-ray absorption spectroscopy and X-ray diffraction investigations during Li uptake and release of a NiFe2O4/CNT composite</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Permien, Stefan; Neumann, Tobias; Indris, Sylvio; Neubueser, Gero; Kienle, Lorenz; Fiedler, Andy; Hansen, Anna-Lena; Gianolio, Diego; Bredow, Thomas; Bensch, Wolfgang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">20</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">28</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19129-19141</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report on results of a comprehensive investigation on reaction mechanisms occurring during Li uptake and release of the composite NiFe2O4/CNT.  Operando X-ray diffraction (XRD) and X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) data collected simultaneously using one in situ cell allowed thorough elucidation of structural and electronic alterations happening during Li uptake.  From the beginning of Li uptake, the Bragg intensity of the spinel reflections decreases which can be explained by redn. of Fe3+ ions and simultaneous movement of the Fe2+ cations from tetrahedral 8a to empty octahedral 16c sites.  The redn. of Fe3+ is clearly evidenced by XAS.  The occupation of tetrahedral sites by Li+ can be excluded based on results of d. functional theory calcns.  Increasing the Li content leads to formation of a new cryst. phase resembling a monoxide with a NaCl-like structure.  The appearance of the new phase is accompanied by a steady decrease of the sizes of coherently scattering domains of the spinel and a growth of the domains of the monoxide phase.  After uptake of about 2.5 Li per NiFe2O4, all Fe3+ cations are reduced to Fe2+ and the tetrahedral 8a sites are empty (XAS spectra).  Careful Rietveld refinements of X-ray powder patterns demonstrate that the tetrahedral 8a site is successively depleted with increasing Li content.  Interestingly, the occupancy of the octahedral 16d site is also slightly reduced.  Increasing the Li content beyond 2.5 Li/NiFe2O4 leads to successive redn. of the cations to very small metal particles embedded in a Li2O matrix (as evidenced by 7Li MAS NMR investigations).  During Li release metallic Ni and Fe are reoxidized to Ni2+ resp.  Fe3+.  The cycling stability of NiFe2O4/CNT is significantly improved compared to pure NiFe2O4 or a mech. mixt. of NiFe2O4 and CNTs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrLagi4gPqvKbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhylv3CzQ0jhg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXht1SmurvF&amp;md5=9d66e2dbf08c7241c5d885fb3ac666d2</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201902485"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bresser, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Transition Metal Oxide Anodes for Electrochemical Energy Storage in Lithium- and Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) with outstanding energy and power d. have been extensively investigated in recent years, rendering them the most suitable energy storage technol. for application in emerging markets such as elec. vehicles and stationary storage.  More recently, sodium, one of the most abundant elements on earth, exhibiting similar physicochem. properties as lithium, has been gaining increasing attention for the development of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) in order to address the concern about Li availability and cost-esp. with regard to stationary applications for which size and vol. of the battery are of less importance.  Compared with traditional intercalation reactions, conversion reaction-based transition metal oxides (TMOs) are prospective anode materials for rechargeable batteries thanks to their low cost and high gravimetric specific capacities.  In this review, the recent progress and remaining challenges of conversion reactions for LIBs and SIBs are discussed, covering an overview about the different synthesis methods, morphol. characteristics, as well as their electrochem. performance.  Potential future research directions and a perspective toward the practical application of TMOs for electrochem. energy storage are also provided.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqAvr0rGrPgY7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhylv3CzQ0jhg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXitFGqu7bL&amp;md5=7093cb0d8eb5176859955db6a466ecbc</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1039/C7QM00175D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiao, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Progress in Conversion Reaction Metal Oxide Anodes for Li-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Mater.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Transition metal oxides (TMOs) based on conversion reactions are attractive candidate anode materials for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) because of their high theor. capacity and safety characteristics.  In this review, we have summarized recent progress in the rational design and efficient synthesis of TMOs with controllable morphologies, compns., and micro-/nanostructures, along with their Li storage behaviors.  Single metal oxides of manganese (Mn), iron (Fe), cobalt (Co), nickel (Ni), copper (Cu), ruthenium (Ru), chromium (Cr), molybdenum (Mo), and tungsten (W) and their common binary metal oxides have been discussed in this review.  Finally, the less well-known merits of conversion reactions are put forward, and the design of metal oxide electrodes making full use of these merits has been proposed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGomTcSlcoMIQbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li2YNCmUwcMzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhslant7nI&amp;md5=4103a7d75bda8f869b1669b1190a659d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1039/C3CP52125G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Klein, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jache, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bhide, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Adelhelm, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Conversion Reactions for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Phys.                         rightTab-cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Conversion reactions for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Klein, Franziska; Jache, Birte; Bhide, Amrtha; Adelhelm, Philipp</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Research on Na-ion batteries is focused on finding suitable electrode materials that enable cell reactions of high energy densities combined with low cost.  Naturally, an assessment of potential electrode materials requires a rational comparison with the analog reaction in Li-ion batteries.  The authors discuss the broad range of different conversion reactions for Na-ion batteries based on their basic thermodn. properties and compare them with their Li analogs.  Capacities, voltages, energy densities and vol. expansions are summarized to sketch out the scope for future studies in this research field.  For a given conversion electrode material, replacing Li by Na leads to a const. shift in cell potential ΔEo(Li-Na) depending on the material class.  For chlorides ΔEo(Li-Na) is nearly zero.  The theor. energy densities of conversion reactions of Na with fluorides or chlorides as pos. electrode materials typically reach values between 700 W h kg-1 and 1000 W h kg-1.  Next to the thermodn. assessment, results on several conversion reactions between Cu compds. (CuS, CuO, CuCl, CuCl2) and Na are being discussed.  Reactions with CuS and CuO were chosen because these compds. are frequently studied for conversion reactions with Li.  Chlorides are interesting because of ΔEo(Li-Na) ≈ 0 V As a result of chloride soly. in the electrolyte, the conversion process proceeds at defined potentials under rather small kinetic limitations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpvFdD49Ctj47Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li2YNCmUwcMzw"> &gt;&gt; The as-prepared TiS2 demonstrates a high reversible capacity of 1040 mA h g(-1) at 0.2 A g(-1) with the capacity contribution of 521 mA h g(-1) in the voltage region below 1.0 V (vs Na/Na(+)), remarkable initial coulombic efficiency of 95.9% and superior rate capability of 621 mA h g(-1) at 40 A g(-1).         </div><div class="casAbstract">As a typical two-dimensional (2D) layered material, (vanadium disulfide) VS2 has huge potentials for application in SIBs due to its large interlayer spacing and high cond. compared to metal oxide or other 2D materials.  Reduced graphene oxide (RGO) possesses exceptional electronic properties and large specific area favoring fast electron transport and rich redox sites.  In this work, VS2 hollow flower spheres and RGO nanocomposites were developed for the first time, it was synthesized using a facile solvothermal method.  Benefiting from the exceptional layered structure, when used as the anode material for SIBs at room temp., the as-prepd. electrode material of VS2 hollow flower spheres @RGO (named as VS2 HFS/RGO) nanocomposites delivers a high reversible discharge specific capacity of around 430 mAh/g at c.d. of 100 mA/g, superior rate performance (2 A/g) and excellent cycling properties with the discharge capacity remained 350 mAh/g at 100 mA/g after 500 cycles.  Results show that the kinetics of VS2 HFS/RGO nanocomposites were mainly a capacitive-controlled storage process and the high capacity contribution were beneficial for good rate performance.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We study the synthesis and electrochem. and mech. performance of layered free-standing papers composed of acid-exfoliated few-layer molybdenum disulfide (MoS2) and reduced graphene oxide (rGO) flakes for use as a self-standing flexible electrode in sodium-ion batteries.         </div><div class="casAbstract">MoS2 nanoflowers with expanded interlayer spacing of the (002) plane were synthesized and used as high-performance anode in Na-ion batteries.  By controlling the cut-off voltage to the range of 0.4-3 V, an intercalation mechanism rather than a conversion reaction is taking place.  The MoS2 nanoflower electrode shows high discharge capacities of 350 mAh g-1 at 0.05 A g-1, 300 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1, and 195 mAh g-1 at 10 A g-1. The stripping of MoS2 layers occurring in charge/discharge cycling contributes to the enhanced kinetics and low energy barrier for the intercalation of Na+ ions.  The electrochem. reaction is mainly controlled by the capacitive process, which facilitates the high-rate capability.  Therefore, MoS2 nanoflowers with expanded interlayers hold promise for rechargeable Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp0lywDrXEibbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lje5LXJcI3htg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhvFShtL3P&amp;md5=218e207e71a96b8802293b3796174e5a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref16" class="rightTabRefNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit16" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701592"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The ternary compd. CuV2S4 exhibits an excellent performance as anode material for sodium ion batteries with a high reversible capacity of 580 mAh g-1 at 0.7 A g-1 after 300 cycles.  A Coulombic efficiency of ≈99% is achieved after the third cycle.  Increase of the C-rate leads to a drop of the capacity, but a full recovery is obsd. after switching back to the initial C-rate.  In the early stages of Na uptake first Cu+ is reduced and expelled from the electrode as nanocryst. metallic Cu. Results of ex-situ X-Ray diffraction expts. performed at different stages of the discharge process demonstrate that at the beginning of Na uptake, Cu+ cations are reduced to nanosized metallic Cu particles which are expelled from the host lattice.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Intercalation-based anode materials for Na-ion batteries show relatively unfavorable electrochem. performances compared with those of Li-ion batteries because of the larger and heavier Na ion, as well as its higher electrode potential.  In contrast, conversion-reaction-based anode materials have great potential for use in Na-ion batteries.  In this study, copper sulfide nanodisks (CuS-NDs) were fabricated by a simple low-temp. reaction and applied as the anode materials for Na-ion batteries with acid-treated single-walled carbon nanotubes (a-SWCNTs), which act as a paperlike nanohybrid. In addn., their electrochem. performances were stable and were maintained over 500 repetitive cycles; this stability arises from the unique nanohybrid structure, in which CuS-NDs are bound together by the a-SWCNT network.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqYOPd13a8xC7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lidvYZn6PueLQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhs1aiurrL&amp;md5=be4eee8132aa5871708fce0cdb96b422</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref21" class="rightTabRefNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit21" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b19138"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Controllable Electrochemical Synthesis of Copper Sulfides as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes with Superior Rate Capability and Ultralong Cycle Life</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are prospective alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale energy-storage applications, owing to the abundant resources of sodium.  Metal sulfides are deemed to be promising anode materials for SIBs due to their low-cost and eco-friendliness.  Herein, for the first time, series of copper sulfides (Cu2S, Cu7S4, and Cu7KS4) are controllably synthesized via a facile electrochem. route in KCl-NaCl-Na2S molten salts.  The as-prepd. Cu2S with micron-sized flakes structure is first investigated as anode of SIBs, which delivers a capacity of 430 mAh g-1 with a high initial Coulombic efficiency of 84.9% at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1.  Moreover, the Cu2S anode demonstrates superior capability (337 mAh g-1 at 20 A g-1, corresponding to 50 C) and ultralong cycle performance (88.2% of capacity retention after 5000 cycles at 5 A g-1, corresponding to 0.0024% of fade rate per cycle). It is noteworthy that the precursor plays a crucial role in the formation of the nanocomposites structure.  SEM, TEM, XRD, IR and Raman spectroscopy are used to investigate the morphol. and structural evolution of CuSNWs/rGO nanocomposites.  The as-fabricated CuSNWs/rGO nanocomposites show remarkably improved Li-storage performance, excellent cycling stability as well as high-rate capability compared with pristine CuS nanowires.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-metal sulfide battery holds great promise for sustainable and cost-effective applications.  Nevertheless, achieving high capacity and cycling stability remains a great challenge.  Here, uniform yolk-shell iron sulfide-carbon nanospheres have been synthesized as cathode materials for the emerging sodium sulfide battery to achieve remarkable capacity of ∼545 mA h g-1 over 100 cycles at 0.2 C (100 mA g-1), delivering ultrahigh energy d. of ∼438 Wh kg-1.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Nanocrystals with quantum-confined length scales are often considered impractical for metal-ion battery electrodes due to the dominance of solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer effects on the measured storage properties.  Here we demonstrate that ultrafine sizes (∼4.5 nm, av.) of iron pyrite, or FeS2, nanoparticles are advantageous to sustain reversible conversion reactions in sodium ion and lithium ion batteries.  This is attributed to a nanoparticle size comparable to or smaller than the diffusion length of Fe during cation exchange, yielding thermodynamically reversible nanodomains of converted Fe metal and NaxS or LixS conversion products.  This is compared to bulk-like electrode materials, where kinetic and thermodn. limitations of surface-nucleated conversion products inhibit successive conversion cycles.  Reversible capacities over 500 and 600 mAh/g for sodium and lithium storage are obsd. for ultrafine nanoparticles, with improved cycling and rate capability.  Unlike alloying or intercalation processes, where SEI effects limit the performance of ultrafine nanoparticles, our work highlights the benefit of quantum dot length-scale nanocrystal electrodes for nanoscale metal sulfide compds. that store energy through chem. conversion reactions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoQ5K7_RKr0B7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lidvYZn6PueLQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhslKlsbbL&amp;md5=04764f41c3057c65e1f434a6b7cca04a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1039/C7NR06886G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wong, K.-W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ng, K.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Hierarchical iron sulfide nanocubes consisting of multiple iron sulfide-carbon core-shell nanoparticles coated with few-layer graphene (Fe1-xS@C/rGO) were prepd. by a two-step in-situ transformation strategy employing Prussian blue (PB) as a starting material. The graphene-coated nanocube structures inhibited the agglomeration of iron sulfide-carbon core-shell nanoparticles and accommodated the huge vol. expansion that occurred during cycling.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Chalcopyrite CuFeS2 with sheet-like or rod-like morphol. was synthesized by solvothermal reaction route in the presence of poly(vinylpyrrolidone) (PVP).  The concn. of PVP has significant influences on the morphol. of CuFeS2 nanocrystals.  With the increasing concns. of PVP, the sheet morphol. of CuFeS2 becomes more uniform.  Meanwhile, the av. dimensions of CuFeS2 sheets are reduced and small CuFeS2 nanoparticles are clearly decreased.  When the molar ratio of CuFeS2 to PVP is 1:2, the shape of CuFeS2 changes to rod-like, which is composed of CuFeS2 sheets.  Considering the influence of uniformity and shape of CuFeS2 nanocrystals, the CuFeS2 nanocrystals with different molar ratios of PVP have been used as active component to prep. anode for Li-ion batteries.  The Li-ion battery using CuFeS2 nanocrystals with the molar ratio of 1:2 to PVP shows the highest reversible specific capacity of ∼519 mA-h/g after 50 cycles at a c.d. of 100 mA/g and maintains a specific capacity of ∼400 mA-h/g at a d. of 1000 mA/g.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Compared to chemosynthetic CuFeS2, natural chalcopyrite (CuFeS2) can be regarded as a promising anode material for exploring ultrafast and stable Li-ion batteries benefiting from it being firsthand, eco-friendly, and resource-rich.  Considering the nonuniform size distribution in it and the fact that homogeneous grain distributions can effectively restrain the aggregation of active materials, the engineering of size is deemed an effective strategy to achieve excellent Li-storage performances.  Herein, varisized natural CuFeS2 are obtained by facial mineral processing technol. and outstanding Li-storage performances are exhibited.  Along with the decreasing of size, the contribution of pseudocapacitive as well as the ion transfer rates are significantly boosted.  As expected, even at 1 A g-1, a remarkable capacity of 1009.7 mA h g-1 is displayed by the sample with the smallest size and most uniform distributions even after 500 cycles.  Furthermore, supported by the detailed anal. of in situ X-ray diffraction and kinetic features, a hybrid of multiple lithium-metal sulfur systems and the major origin of the enhanced capacity upon long cycles are confirmed.  Remarkably, this work is expected to increase the far-ranging applications of natural chalcopyrite as a firsthand anode material for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and inform the readers about the effects of particle size on Li-storage performances.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqAkwoi-a1iIrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liKREEeZxBpug"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXpvFKitw%253D%253D&amp;md5=99122fc2f39ea407104d2086db807360</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref34" class="rightTabRefNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit34" data-doi="10.1107/S1600576717004708"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Filik, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ashton, A. Spherical absorption corrections and anomalous dispersion data were applied in the refinement of the S x coordinate at site 8d (0.2574 (2), 1/4, 1/8] and the anisotropic temp. factors.  The thermal motion of all atoms is essentially isotropic and agrees, with that of equiv. atoms in cubanite, CuFe2S3. Reassessment of the published spectra derived by these methods produces consistent results and reveals the 'd count' in the copper compds. to be intermediate between d9 and d10.         </div><div class="casAbstract">It is desirable to develop electrode materials for advanced rechargeable batteries with low cost, long life, and high-rate capability.  Pyrite FeS2, as an easily obtained natural mineral, has been already commercialized in primary lithium batteries, but encountered problems in rechargeable batteries with carbonate-based electrolytes due to the limited cycle life caused by the conversion-type reaction (FeS2 + 4M → Fe + 2M2S (M = Li or Na)).  Herein, we demonstrate that FeS2 microspheres can be applied in room-temp. rechargeable sodium batteries with only the intercalation reaction by simultaneously selecting a compatible NaSO3CF3/diglyme electrolyte and tuning the cut-off voltage to 0.8 V.  A surprisingly high-rate capability (170 mA h g-1 at 20 A g-1) and unprecedented long-term cyclability (∼90% capacity retention for 20 000 cycles) has been obtained. Moreover, 18650-type sodium batteries were constructed exhibiting a high capacity of ∼4200 mA h (corresponding to 126 W h kg-1 and 382 W h L-1) and a capacity retention of 97% after an initial 200 cycles at 4 A during charge-discharge.  This shows that the prodn. of rechargeable sodium batteries with FeS2 microspheres is viable for com. utilization.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrFxqeM-EGKh7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhnGjfFVzG2qw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhs1arsL0%253D&amp;md5=25c3d019d88687effcc5109727ee9bce</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref42" class="rightTabRefNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit42" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.76.115413"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Masadeh, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In light of the impeding depletion of fossil fuels and necessity to lower carbon dioxide emissions, economically viable high-performance batteries are urgently needed for numerous applications ranging from elec. cars to stationary large-scale electricity storage. Stanley; Grey, Clare P.; Amatucci, Glenn G.; Zhu, Yi-Mei; Graetz, Jason</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">133</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">46</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">18828-18836</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Materials that undergo a conversion reaction with lithium (e.g., metal fluorides MF2: M = Fe, Cu, ...) often accommodate more than one Li atom per transition-metal cation, and are promising candidates for high-capacity cathodes for lithium ion batteries.  However, little is known about the mechanisms involved in the conversion process, the origins of the large polarization during electrochem. cycling, and why some materials are reversible (e.g., FeF2) while others are not (e.g., CuF2).  The authors studied the conversion reaction of binary metal fluorides, FeF2 and CuF2, using local and bulk probes to better understand the mechanisms underlying their contrasting electrochem. behavior.  X-ray pair-distribution-function and magnetization measurements were used to det. changes in short-range ordering, particle size and microstructure, while high-resoln. TEM and EELS were used to measure the at.-level structure of individual particles and map the phase distribution in the initial and fully lithiated electrodes.  Both FeF2 and CuF2 react with lithium via a direct conversion process with no intercalation step, but there are differences in the conversion process and final phase distribution.  During the reaction of Li+ with FeF2, small metallic iron nanoparticles ( &lt; 5 nm in diam.) nucleate in close proximity to the converted LiF phase, as a result of the low diffusivity of iron.  The iron nanoparticles are interconnected and form a bicontinuous network, which provides a pathway for local electron transport through the insulating LiF phase.  The massive interface formed between nanoscale solid phases provides a pathway for ionic transport during the conversion process.  These results offer the 1st exptl. evidence explaining the origins of the high lithium reversibility in FeF2.  In contrast to FeF2, no continuous Cu network was obsd. in the lithiated CuF2; rather, the converted Cu segregates to large particles (5-12 nm in diam.) during the 1st discharge, which may be partially responsible for the lack of reversibility in the CuF2 electrode.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGomRjMuzUHJ2LVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhnGjfFVzG2qw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1WksL7I&amp;md5=8b1937b1bb2422c104a24b9a0e8fa01b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1021/ic50138a009"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">DiGiuseppe, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Steger, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wold, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kostiner, E.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Preparation and Characterization of the System Copper Gallium Iron Sulfide (CuGa<sub>1-X</sub>Fe<sub>x</sub>S<sub>2</sub>)</span>. <i>Inorg. It is noteworthy that the replacement of S by Se in the chalcopyrite decreases the strength of both the superexchange interaction and the interat. binding.  As the interat. distance increases by 1%, the magnetic hyperfine field and the Debye temp. decrease by 0.8 and 6%, resp.  The Fe ions are in ferric states.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGolPs7niTT0erVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhnGjfFVzG2qw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK2MXht1yqu7k%253D&amp;md5=6eed33f6ba11359e0fed20724cafe7cf</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref47" class="rightTabRefNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit47" data-doi="10.1016/j.jelechem.2020.114523"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Okabe, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Urita, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Moriguchi, I.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Cu<sub>2</sub>S Hollow Spheres as an Anode for High-Rate Sodium Storage Performance</span>. <i>J. Electroanal. Chem.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>874</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">114523</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jelechem.2020.114523</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Cu2S hollow spheres as an anode for high-rate sodium storage performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jianbiao; Okabe, Jugo; Urita, Koki; Moriguchi, Isamu; Wei, Mingdeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Electroanalytical Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">874</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">114523</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JECHES</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1873-2569</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cu2S hollow spheres composed of nanoparticles were synthesized via a template-free hydrothermal process in the absence of additive.  The obtained Cu2S hollow spheres not only provide a large surface for contacting between electrode and the electrolyte, but also keep sufficient space for the vol. variation during the sodiation/desodiation process.  Consequently, as an anode for Na ion battery, the Cu2S hollow spheres deliver a large capacity of 369 mA h g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.2 A g-1 and demonstrated superior rate performance (331.7 mA h g-1 at 0.5 A g-1, 319 mA h g-1 at 4 A g-1) and long-term cycling stability (343 mA h g-1 at 2 A g-1 after 1000 cycles).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrrIY_cXbRNNrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liu6fYO_RQAeA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhsFWhs7%252FI&amp;md5=8be649a6ee88ef8bff460bf5d3a4ca32</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref48" class="rightTabRefNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit48" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b18864"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhong, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Interpreting Abnormal Charge–Discharge Plateau Migration in Cu<sub>x</sub>S during Long-Term Cycling</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>11</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3961</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3970</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsami.8b18864</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interpreting Abnormal Charge-Discharge Plateau Migration in CuxS during Long-Term Cycling</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Zuguang; Chen, Ting; Wu, Chunjin; Qu, Jie; Wu, Zhenguo; Guo, Xiaodong; Zhong, Benhe; Liu, Huakun; Dou, Shixue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3961-3970</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Voltage polarization during cycling, the charge potential increase of anode or discharge plateau decrease of cathode, is widely obsd. and would lower the output voltage.  Conversely, an anomalous potential plateau neg. migration phenomenon was obsd. in CuxS anode of Na-ion battery.  The background mechanism was clarified from the switch of intercalation-conversion reactions and structure evolution.  The dynamic cooperation between intercalation and conversion reactions may root the potential plateau neg. migration during cycling.  In the initial stage, the intercalation-type reaction with Na3Cu4S4 and Na4Cu2S3 products at 2.13 and 1.92 V would dominate the early migration process of potential plateaus.  In the 2nd stage, the conversion-type reaction dominated by Na2S and metallic Cu formed at 1.85 and 1.53 V in the later period.  The aforementioned results would provide new perspective on the electrochem. behavior of transition-metal sulfide anode and provide a clue for reducing voltage polarization.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrEG9-ntnLFlLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liu6fYO_RQAeA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXkslKitA%253D%253D&amp;md5=4e44968872488cbf7f4cf0d142249ddf</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i25">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c04946?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c04946</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Tables of refinement parameters of all Rietveld and PDF refinements; <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> improvement, determined copper reflection intensities, and results of EDX measurements; cyclic voltammograms of the first five cycles of CuFeS<sub>2</sub>; figures from Rietveld and PDF fits; and the full view of the <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR spectra of CuFeS<sub>2</sub> for different discharge/charge states (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c04946/suppl_file/am1c04946_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c04946/suppl_file/am1c04946_si_001.pdf">am1c04946_si_001.pdf (2.15 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){

	}); 



})();

<script nonce="90da876819cfb325-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da876819cfb325-SJC">
"use strict"

	/* 
	Create an in-page pop-up message, only for the InfoCentral homepage, that will gather email registrants for the library marketing list.
	 
	Needs the end point
	*/

var infoCentralCapture = (function() {

    //Detect mobile device
    window.mobilecheck = function() {
        var check = false;
        (function(a){if(/(android|bb\d+|meego).+mobile|avantgo|bada\/|blackberry|blazer|compal|elaine|fennec|hiptop|iemobile|ip(hone|od)|iris|kindle|lge |maemo|midp|mmp|mobile.+firefox|netfront|opera u|g560|gene|gf\-5|g\-mo|go(\.w|od)|gr(ad|un)|haie|hcit|hd\-(m|p|t)|hei\-|hi(pt|ta)|hp( i|ip)|hs\-c|ht(c(\-| |_|a|g|p|s|t)|tp)|hu(aw|tc)|i\-(20|go|ma)|i230|iac( |\-|\/)|ibro|idea|ig01|ikom|im1k|inno|ipaq|iris|ja(t|v)a|jbro|jemu|jigs|kddi|keji|kgt( |\/)|klon|kpt |kwc\-|kyo(c|k)|le(no|xi)|lg( g|\/(k|l|u)|50|54|\-[a-w])|libw|lynx|m1\-w|m3ga|m50\/|ma(te|ui|xo)|mc(01|21|ca)|m\-cr|me(rc|ri)|mi(o8|oa|ts)|mmef|mo(01|02|bi|de|do|t(\-| |o|v)|zz)|mt(50|p1|v )|mwbp|mywa|n10[0-2]|n20[2-3]|n30(0|2)|n50(0|2|5)|n7(0(0|1)|10)|ne((c|m)\-|on|tf|wf|wg|wt)|nok(6|i)|nzph|o2im|op(ti|wv)|oran|owg1|p800|pan(a|d|t)|pdxg|pg(13|\-([1-8]|c))|phil|pire|pl(ay|uc)|pn\-2|po(ck|rt|se)|prox|psio|pt\-g|qa\-a|qc(07|12|21|32|60|\-[2-7]|i\-)|qtek|r380|r600|raks|rim9|ro(ve|zo)|s55\/|sa(ge|ma|mm|ms|ny|va)|sc(01|h\-|oo|p\-)|sdk\/|se(c(\-|0|1)|47|mc|nd|ri)|sgh\-|shar|sie(\-|m)|sk\-0|sl(45|id)|sm(al|ar|b3|it|t5)|so(ft|ny)|sp(01|h\-|v\-|v )|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");
    $.getJSON('https://extreme-ip-lookup.com/json/', function(data, status) {
            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");
                });
                
            } else {
                    cookie=status;
            }

            cookie1 = cookie;
            cookie = cookie1 + "; path=/; domain=.acs.org";
            //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;
            $.cookie("ACSPubs2", btoa(cookie), {path: '/'}); // Session cookie valid across the entire site.
            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging
            console.log("ACSPubs2 success");
    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
        cookie="error: 127.0.0.1";
        //document.cookie = "ACSPubs2= "+ btoa(cookie) ;

    });
});
